import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs matrix multiplication, max pooling, sum, and scaling.
    """
    def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, kernel_size, scale_factor):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.matmul = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)
        self.max_pool = nn.MaxPool1d(kernel_size)
        self.scale_factor = scale_factor

    def forward(self, x):
        """
        Args:
            x (torch.Tensor): Input tensor of shape (batch_size, in_features).

        Returns:
            torch.Tensor: Output tensor of shape (batch_size, out_features).
        """
        x = self.matmul(x)
        x = self.max_pool(x.unsqueeze(1)).squeeze(1)
        x = torch.sum(x, dim=1)
        x = x * self.scale_factor
        return x

batch_size = 128
in_features = 32768
out_features = 32768
kernel_size = 2
scale_factor = 0.5

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_features)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_features, out_features, kernel_size, scale_factor]

The challenging part is that we need to keep accumulators for all pairs and all batch elements within a tile. For 256 pairs and 128 batch elements with 2 accumulators each: 256 * 128 * 2 * 4B = 262KB That's a reasonable amount to keep in registers distributed across threads. If we have 256 threads per block, each thread could handle 128 batch elements / 256 threads = 0.5 batch elements... no wait. Let's say we use thread blocks of 256 threads. Total weight reads: 32768 * 16MB = 512GB. But the weights are only 4GB! So we're reading weights 128 times (once per batch element). That's exactly what happens in a standard GEMM too - weights are reused across batch elements in caches. In a standard GEMM, all batch elements share the same weight matrix. The GPU processes the weights in tiles, and each tile is read once and used for all batch elements that need it. In my proposed scheme, each block only handles one batch element, so weights are not reused across batch elements at the block level. The L2 cache might help, but with 64 streaming multiprocessors and 50MB L2, and each block reading 16MB, we'd quickly evict data. So we need to design the kernel so that threads within a block or across blocks cooperate to load weights once and use them for multiple batch elements. For each output pair j, and each batch element b: y_{2j}^{(b)} = sum_k x_{b,k} * W_{2j,k} + b_{2j} y_{2j+1}^{(b)} = sum_k x_{b,k} * W_{2j+1,k} + b_{2j+1} contribution = max(y_{2j}^{(b)}, y_{2j+1}^{(b)}) This means for each pair j, we need to compute two dot products for each batch element. But notice that the weights for pair j (rows 2j and 2j+1) are the SAME across all batch elements. Only x differs by batch. So we can structure the computation as: For each pair j: For each input chunk: Load W[2j, chunk] and W[2j+1, chunk] into registers/shared memory For each batch element b: dot0 = x[b] · W[2j] dot1 = x[b] · W[2j+1] accumulate max(dot0+bias0, dot1+bias1) into result[b] If we process 64 pairs at a time: Load W for 64*2=128 rows For each batch element (128 total): Compute 64 dot products (actually 64*2=128 dot products) Reduce Total FMAs: 128 batch * 64 pairs * 32768 * 2 = 5.3B per tile Number of tiles: 16384/64 = 256 Total: same 137B FMAs But the memory access: for each tile, read 128 rows * 32768 * 4B = 16MB of weights. 256 tiles * 16MB = 4GB total weight reads. That's perfect! We read each weight exactly once. For x: each batch element is read once per tile. 128 batch * 32768 * 4B = 16MB per tile, 256 tiles = 4GB of x reads. But x is only 16MB! So if x stays in L2 or shared memory across tiles, we can read it much less. Actually, if we keep x in shared memory or L2 across tiles, we'd only read it once. x fits easily in L2 (50MB). L2 cache on H100 is large enough to hold the entire 16MB input. But for the x value, if all 128 batch elements are processed by the same SM in shared memory, we still need multiple blocks. Let me think about this tile-based approach more carefully: Block design: - Process 64 pairs for all 128 batch elements - 128 batch elements * 32768 features * 2 rows * 4B / 2 = wait, let's compute For one block handling 64 pairs: - Accumulate 128 dot products per batch element * 64 pairs * 2 (rows) = 16384 floats per batch element. - Since we only need the max and sum, we can accumulate incrementally: For each input feature k: For each batch element b: xval = x[b, k] For each pair j in 64: dot0 += xval * W[2j, k] dot1 += xval * W[2j+1, k] Wait, we can't just accumulate a single dot0 for all pairs. Each pair has its own dot0. So we need to maintain 64 * 2 = 128 accumulators per batch element. That's 128 * 128 batch = 16384 floats = 64KB per block if we keep them in shared memory. Or we can distribute them across threads. If a block has 256 threads, each thread could handle 128 batch / 256 = 0.5 batch elements? Not ideal. Very manageable in registers or shared memory. Number of blocks: 128 / 32 = 4 batch blocks + 16384/64 = 256 pair blocks? No, this is a 2D grid. Great. Memory access: - For a warp (32 threads), how are they organized? A warp is the first 32 threads in linear thread order. If W_smem is stored as W_smem[128][CHUNK_SIZE], then for fixed k=0: Thread 0 reads W_smem[0][0] = &W_smem[0][0] Thread 1 reads W_smem[2][0] = &W_smem[0][0] + 2*CHUNK_SIZE Thread 2 reads W_smem[4][0] = &W_smem[0][0] + 4*CHUNK_SIZE ... I need to rethink the shared memory layout. If multiple threads need to read different rows for the same column, and the rows are strided, we might not get maximum bandwidth. Alternative: transpose W in shared memory. Store W_smem[CHUNK_SIZE][128] (chunk_size x rows). Then for column k: W_smem[k][0], W_smem[k][1], W_smem[k][2]... are consecutive. And thread 16 also reads W_smem[k][0] etc., which duplicates but from SMEM it's fast. But loading W from global memory to this transposed SMEM layout: we need to read W_global[row][col] and write to W_smem[col][row]. This is a transpose operation during the load. For global memory coalescing when loading W: W_global is [32768][32768], row-major. If threads read consecutive columns for the same row, that's coalesced. For the load, we can have threads read in a coalesced pattern: for (int i = threadIdx; i < 128 * CHUNK_SIZE; i += blockDim.x*blockDim.y) { int row = i / CHUNK_SIZE; int col = i % CHUNK_SIZE; int global_row = block_pair_start * 2 + row; int global_col = chunk_start + col; W_smem[col][row] = W[global_row * in_features + global_col]; } Since i increments by 1 across threads, and i maps to consecutive elements in row-major order (row * CHUNK_SIZE + col), this is coalesced. Now let me think about the overall code structure: ```cuda template <int CHUNK_SIZE> __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, // [batch, in_features] const float* __restrict__ W, // [out_features, in_features] const float* __restrict__ bias, // [out_features] float* __restrict__ result, // [batch] int batch_size, int in_features, int out_features, float scale_factor ) { const int PAIRS_PER_BLOCK = 64; const int BATCH_PER_BLOCK = 32; int batch_tile = blockIdx.x; // 0..3 (since 128/32=4) int pair_tile = blockIdx.y; // 0..255 (since 16384/64=256) int pair_base = pair_tile * PAIRS_PER_BLOCK; int batch_base = batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK; // Shared memory: W_smem[128 rows][CHUNK_SIZE+1], x_smem[32][CHUNK_SIZE+1] // But need 128 rows = 2*PAIRS_PER_BLOCK extern __shared__ float smem[]; float (*W_smem)[CHUNK_SIZE+1] = (float (*)[CHUNK_SIZE+1])smem; float (*x_smem)[CHUNK_SIZE+1] = (float (*)[CHUNK_SIZE+1])&smem[128 * (CHUNK_SIZE+1)]; float accum[2][8]; // 2 batch elements * 4 pairs = 8 slots, each with 2 accumulators // wait, each thread handles 2 batch and 4 pairs, so 8 pairs total? For threads 0..15, `__shfl_down(val0, 16)` would get 0 from threads 16..31. That's fine for sum. But threads 16..31 would get values from threads beyond the warp, which is 0. So if we apply the same reduction to all threads, the result is: - Thread 0 gets sum of val0 from threads 0..15, plus 0 from threads 16..31. - Thread 1 gets same. ... - Thread 16 gets sum of val1 from threads 16..31. That's wrong! We don't want to mix different batch elements. So we need to do two separate reductions in each warp, partitioning the warp. For batch element reduction, we want: - Reduce across px=0..15 for a fixed by value - tid in warp: by_warp = tid / 16 wait, tid = by*16 + px. So for a fixed by, tid ranges from by*16 to by*16+15. - These are consecutive 16 threads. We can use a loop: for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val0 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFF0000, val0, offset); // no wait } Actually, the easier way is to note that for a 16-thread subgroup within a warp (threads with same by), they map to tid by*16 + 0 to by*16 + 15. Wait, tid = by * 16 + px. For by=0: tid=0..15. For by=1: tid=16..31. If we just use __shfl_down_sync on the full warp, the threads for by=0 and by=1 are interleaved in a specific way (actually not interleaved, they're contiguous blocks of 16). To reduce within the 16 threads for by=0: Use mask 0x0000FFFF for tid 0..15, and 0xFFFF0000 for tid 16..31. But shuffle operations read from target lane. `__shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset)` means lane i reads from lane i+offset. For tid=0 (by=0, px=0): val0 should reduce with tid=1..15 (also by=0) For tid=16 (by=1, px=0): val0 should reduce with tid=17..31 (also by=1) If we use: val0 += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val0, 1); For tid=0: reads from tid=1 (same by=0). Good. For tid=1: reads from tid=2 (same by=0). Good. For tid=15: reads from tid=16 (different by!). Bad. So offset=1 crosses the boundary between by=0 and by=1. For a subgroup of 16 contiguous threads, we can use a mask: For tid in 0..15, use mask where only 0..15 participate. But `__shfl_down_sync` reads from lane i+offset regardless of mask. The mask just says which threads are active. If tid=15 tries to read from tid=16, and tid=16 is not in the mask, it returns the fallback value. Actually, the mask specifies which threads are participating in the shuffle. `__shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset)`: - For each active thread in mask, lane i gets value from lane i+offset. - If lane i+offset is not in mask or not active, the result is undefined? No, actually if i+offset is out of range or not in the mask, the result is the same as lane i's original input for some implementations, or zero. To be safe, we can use the fact that 32 is a power of 2, but our groups are 16. Let me use a simpler approach. Actually, we can avoid warp-level reduction complexity by not doing local_sum in shared memory. Alternative: at the end of all chunks, each thread has 16 accumulators. We can write them to shared memory and then do a block-level reduction. SMEM for reduction: we need space for 32 batch elements * 64 pairs * 2 rows? No, after adding bias and taking max, each thread has 2 floats (local_sum[0], local_sum[1]) for 2 batch elements. Total per block: 32 floats. That's tiny. All threads with threadIdx.y=5 handle batch_base+10 in their local_sum[0]. There are 16 such threads (px=0..15). And for batch_base + 11: by=5, sb=1. 16 threads. So we need to reduce 16 values across px=0..15 for each (by, sb) combination. We can do this with warp shuffles since 16 threads fit in half a warp. For simplicity, I'll do a 2D shared memory reduction: ```cuda __shared__ float s_reduce[32]; // One per batch element in block // Each warp handles one by. But a warp has 32 threads with by=0 and by=1. // Actually, two warps handle the full block of 16x16 (since 256 threads / 32 = 8 warps). // Warp 0: tid=0..31 = by=0,1 with px=0..15 // Warp 1: tid=32..63 = by=2,3 with px=0..15 // ... // Warp 7: tid=224..255 = by=14,15 with px=0..15 // For each warp, we can reduce the two batch elements it contains. // For warp w (by=w*2 and by=w*2+1): // Reduce local_sum[0] across px=0..15 for by=w*2 // Reduce local_sum[1] across px=0..15 for by=w*2+1 ``` For a warp, we can split it into two halves of 16 threads each. Threads in lower half (lane 0..15): by = warp_id * 2 Threads in upper half (lane 16..31): by = warp_id * 2 + 1 For local_sum[0] in lower half, we need to reduce across lane 0..15. For local_sum[1] in upper half, reduce across lane 16..31. We can do this with a butterfly shuffle: ```cuda // First, put the appropriate value into a register int lane_id = threadIdx.x + threadIdx.y * 16; // Actually tid = threadIdx.y * 16 + threadIdx.x int lane = tid % 32; int warp_id = tid / 32; int my_by = warp_id * 2 + (lane >= 16 ? 1 : 0); float my_val = (lane < 16) ? local_sum[0] : local_sum[1]; // Now reduce my_val across the appropriate half-warp #pragma unroll for (int offset = 8; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { my_val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, my_val, offset); } // After this, lane 0 has sum of lanes 0..15, lane 16 has sum of lanes 16..31 if (lane == 0 || lane == 16) { int batch_idx = batch_base + my_by * 2 + (lane >= 16 ? 1 : 0); // Wait, I need to be careful. // For lane < 16: my_by = warp_id * 2, and local_sum[0] = sum for batch_base + my_by*2 + 0? No. } ``` This is getting complicated with indexing. Let me simplify the thread-to-batch mapping. Shuffle doesn't easily support non-power-of-2 or non-full-warp groups unless we use masks carefully. Alternative: shared memory reduction. ```cuda __shared__ float s_sum[32]; // 32 batch elements s_sum[batch_in_block] = 0; __syncthreads(); // Each thread computes local_sum for its 8 pairs atomicAdd(&s_sum[batch_in_block], local_sum); __syncthreads(); // Then one thread per batch element writes to global result if (threadIdx.x == 0) { // some condition result[global_batch] = s_sum[batch_in_block] * scale; } ``` Atomic add to shared memory is very fast (basically register speed on modern GPUs for low contention). With 8 threads per batch element atomically adding to the same shared memory location, there will be some contention but with only 8 threads it's minimal. Let's quantify: 8 threads in a warp doing atomicAdd to the same shared memory bank. On modern GPUs, shared memory atomics are quite fast, especially for adds. 8-way contention on the same address: the hardware can handle this with replay, but it might take a few cycles. Since we only do this once per block (not in a loop), the overhead is negligible. Good! So we can use shared memory atomics for the block-level reduction. Block-level code: ```cuda __shared__ float s_sum[32]; if (threadIdx.x < 32) s_sum[threadIdx.x] = 0.0f; __syncthreads(); // thread tid handles batch = tid / 8, pairs starting at (tid % 8) * 8 int batch_in_block = tid / 8; int pair_start_in_block = (tid % 8) * 8; // ... compute local_sum for these 8 pairs ... atomicAdd(&s_sum[batch_in_block], local_sum); __syncthreads(); if (tid < 32 && tid < batch_elements_in_this_block) { // tid corresponds to batch_in_block int global_batch = batch_base + tid; result[global_batch] = s_sum[tid] * scale_factor; } ``` Wait, but `tid < batch_elements_in_this_block`. Threads 8 and 24 both access bank (16+c)%32. Conflict! 4-way conflict -> 4x slowdown for those accesses. That's not great but might be acceptable. But wait, are they in the same warp? Actually, since multiple threads read the exact same address in SMEM, this should be handled by broadcast on modern GPUs. For shared memory, when multiple threads in a warp read the same address, it should be broadcast without bank conflicts! Wait, is that true? Yes, for shared memory, if all threads in a warp read the same address, it's a broadcast and takes one cycle. If multiple threads read the same bank but different addresses, it's a conflict. But if they read the EXACT SAME address, it's broadcast. tid=0 and tid=8 both read W_smem[0][c] and W_smem[1][c]. These are the exact same addresses. So the hardware should broadcast them. Same for tid=16 and tid=24. Similarly, tid=1,9,17,25 all read W_smem[8][c] and W_smem[9][c] - exact same addresses. Broadcast. So the W_smem reads are actually broadcasts, not conflicts! This is perfect! The SMEM access pattern is almost entirely broadcasts. Very efficient. Let me re-read the model more carefully: ```python class Model(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_features, out_features, kernel_size, scale_factor): super(Model, self).__init__() self.matmul = nn.Linear(in_features, out_features) self.max_pool = nn.MaxPool1d(kernel_size) self.scale_factor = scale_factor def forward(self, x): x = self.matmul(x) x = self.max_pool(x.unsqueeze(1)).squeeze(1) x = torch.sum(x, dim=1) x = x * self.scale_factor return x ``` Important: `self.matmul` is `nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)`. The actual weight shape in PyTorch for nn.Linear is `[out_features, in_features]`, but the internal representation uses `weight` of shape `[out_features, in_features]` and computes `x @ weight.T + bias` (or `F.linear(x, weight, bias)`). Wait, in PyTorch, `nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)` stores `weight` as `[out_features, in_features]`. The forward is `x @ weight.t()`. So the weight is conceptually transposed. In the reference: `x` has shape `[batch, in_features]`. `self.matmul(x)` does `x @ W^T + b` where W has shape `[out_features, in_features]`. So the computation is: output[b, j] = sum_i x[b, i] * W[j, i] + bias[j] But if W in PyTorch is stored as `[out_features, in_features]`, then W[j, i] is the element at row j, column i. So yes, my earlier understanding was correct. However, I need to double check: does PyTorch's nn.Linear use weight.T in the forward? Actually, no. PyTorch stores the weight matrix as `[out_features, in_features]` but computes `F.linear(input, weight, bias)` which is mathematically equivalent to `input @ weight.t()`. But the actual implementation might use either `input @ weight.t()` or `weight @ input.t()` depending on the backend. For cuBLAS, it might call `gemm` with transposed or non-transposed arguments. Let me write the full kernel: ```cuda template <int CHUNK_SIZE> __global__ void fused_linear_maxpool_sum_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int in_features, int out_features, float scale_factor ) { // Block configuration: process BATCH_PER_BLOCK batch elements and PAIRS_PER_BLOCK pairs const int BATCH_PER_BLOCK = 32; const int PAIRS_PER_BLOCK = 64; const int NUM_PAIR_GROUPS = 8; // PAIRS_PER_BLOCK / pairs_per_thread = 64/8 = 8 int batch_tile = blockIdx.x; int pair_tile = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int batch_in_block = tid / NUM_PAIR_GROUPS; int pair_group = tid % NUM_PAIR_GROUPS; int batch_idx = batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK + batch_in_block; int pair_base = pair_tile * PAIRS_PER_BLOCK; int pair_start = pair_group * 8; if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return; // Shared memory extern __shared__ float smem[]; // W_smem: [PAIRS_PER_BLOCK * 2][CHUNK_SIZE + 1] - 128 rows of weights const int W_SMEM_STRIDE = CHUNK_SIZE + 1; float* W_smem = smem; // x_smem: [BATCH_PER_BLOCK][CHUNK_SIZE + 1] - 32 batch elements float* x_smem = &smem[PAIRS_PER_BLOCK * 2 * W_SMEM_STRIDE]; const int X_SMEM_STRIDE = CHUNK_SIZE + 1; // Accumulators: 8 pairs * 2 rows = 16 floats float acc[16]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < 16; ++i) acc[i] = 0.0f; int num_chunks = in_features / CHUNK_SIZE; for (int chunk = 0; chunk < num_chunks; ++chunk) { int chunk_offset = chunk * CHUNK_SIZE; // Load W tile into shared memory int W_elements = PAIRS_PER_BLOCK * 2 * CHUNK_SIZE; #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < W_elements; i += blockDim.x) { int row = i / CHUNK_SIZE; int col = i - row * CHUNK_SIZE; // i % CHUNK_SIZE int global_row = pair_base * 2 + row; int global_col = chunk_offset + col; W_smem[row * W_SMEM_STRIDE + col] = W[global_row * in_features + global_col]; } // Load x tile into shared memory int x_elements = BATCH_PER_BLOCK * CHUNK_SIZE; #pragma unroll for (int i = tid; i < x_elements; i += blockDim.x) { int b = i / CHUNK_SIZE; int col = i - b * CHUNK_SIZE; int global_b = batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK + b; if (global_b < batch_size) { x_smem[b * X_SMEM_STRIDE + col] = x[global_b * in_features + global_col]; } else { x_smem[b * X_SMEM_STRIDE + col] = 0.0f; } } __syncthreads(); // Check if valid batch if (batch_idx < batch_size) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < CHUNK_SIZE; ++c) { float xval = x_smem[batch_in_block * X_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; #pragma unroll for (int p = 0; p < 8; ++p) { int row0 = (pair_start + p) * 2; int row1 = row0 + 1; float w0 = W_smem[row0 * W_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; float w1 = W_smem[row1 * W_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; acc[p * 2] += xval * w0; acc[p * 2 + 1] += xval * w1; } } } __syncthreads(); } // Apply bias, max, and reduce __shared__ float s_sum[BATCH_PER_BLOCK]; if (threadIdx.x < BATCH_PER_BLOCK) s_sum[threadIdx.x] = 0.0f; __syncthreads(); if (batch_idx < batch_size) { float local_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int p = 0; p < 8; ++p) { int pair_idx = pair_base + pair_start + p; if (pair_idx * 2 < out_features) { float y0 = acc[p * 2] + bias[pair_idx * 2]; float y1 = acc[p * 2 + 1] + bias[pair_idx * 2 + 1]; local_sum += fmaxf(y0, y1); } } atomicAdd(&s_sum[batch_in_block], local_sum); } __syncthreads(); if (tid < BATCH_PER_BLOCK && batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK + tid < batch_size) { int global_batch = batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK + tid; out[global_batch] = s_sum[tid] * scale_factor; } } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue with the atomicAdd and __syncthreads usage. All threads must reach __syncthreads(). If some threads return early (e.g., `if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return;` at the beginning), then threads that reach __syncthreads later might deadlock. In the code above, I put `if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return;` at the top, before any __syncthreads. And then inside the chunk loop, I guard with `if (batch_idx < batch_size)` around the compute but NOT around the __syncthreads. That should be fine because all threads reach __syncthreads regardless. But wait, I have `if (batch_idx >= batch_size) return;` at the top. This means threads with invalid batch_idx return immediately and never reach the __syncthreads at the end of the chunk loop. This would cause deadlock! In CUDA, if any thread in a block executes a `return`, it doesn't necessarily break __syncthreads, but if some threads return and others hit __syncthreads, it's undefined behavior and typically deadlocks. So I need to remove the early return and instead have all threads participate in loading shared memory, while only valid batch threads do the compute. Let me restructure: ```cuda bool valid = (batch_idx < batch_size); // ... all threads participate in loading SMEM ... __syncthreads(); if (valid) { // compute } __syncthreads(); ``` This way all threads reach both __syncthreads. Also, I should be careful about the number of pair tiles. If out_features is odd, pair_tiles should be (out_features + 1) / 2, and the last tile might have fewer valid pairs. I added a check `if (pair_idx * 2 < out_features)` which handles this. Let me also think about performance. `num_chunks = in_features / CHUNK_SIZE`. If in_features is not divisible by CHUNK_SIZE, we'd need a remainder loop. For the specific problem, 32768 / 128 = 256, exact. Let me draft it: ```python import torch from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = """ template <int CHUNK_SIZE> __global__ void fused_kernel( const float* __restrict__ x, const float* __restrict__ W, const float* __restrict__ bias, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int in_features, int out_features, float scale_factor ) { const int BATCH_PER_BLOCK = 32; const int PAIRS_PER_BLOCK = 64; const int PAIRS_PER_THREAD = 8; const int NUM_PAIR_GROUPS = PAIRS_PER_BLOCK / PAIRS_PER_THREAD; // 8 int batch_tile = blockIdx.x; int pair_tile = blockIdx.y; int tid = threadIdx.x; int batch_in_block = tid / NUM_PAIR_GROUPS; int pair_group = tid % NUM_PAIR_GROUPS; int batch_idx = batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK + batch_in_block; int pair_base = pair_tile * PAIRS_PER_BLOCK; int pair_start = pair_group * PAIRS_PER_THREAD; bool valid = (batch_idx < batch_size); const int W_SMEM_STRIDE = CHUNK_SIZE + 1; const int X_SMEM_STRIDE = CHUNK_SIZE + 1; extern __shared__ float smem[]; float* W_smem = smem; float* x_smem = &smem[PAIRS_PER_BLOCK * 2 * W_SMEM_STRIDE]; float* s_sum = &x_smem[BATCH_PER_BLOCK * X_SMEM_STRIDE]; float acc[PAIRS_PER_THREAD * 2]; #pragma unroll for (int i = 0; i < PAIRS_PER_THREAD * 2; ++i) { acc[i] = 0.0f; } int num_chunks = in_features / CHUNK_SIZE; for (int chunk = 0; chunk < num_chunks; ++chunk) { int chunk_offset = chunk * CHUNK_SIZE; // Load W into shared memory int W_total = PAIRS_PER_BLOCK * 2 * CHUNK_SIZE; #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = tid; i < W_total; i += blockDim.x) { int row = i / CHUNK_SIZE; int col = i - row * CHUNK_SIZE; int global_row = pair_base * 2 + row; int global_col = chunk_offset + col; float val = 0.0f; if (global_row < out_features && global_col < in_features) { val = W[global_row * in_features + global_col]; } W_smem[row * W_SMEM_STRIDE + col] = val; } // Load x into shared memory int x_total = BATCH_PER_BLOCK * CHUNK_SIZE; #pragma unroll 4 for (int i = tid; i < x_total; i += blockDim.x) { int b = i / CHUNK_SIZE; int col = i - b * CHUNK_SIZE; int global_b = batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK + b; float val = 0.0f; if (global_b < batch_size && chunk_offset + col < in_features) { val = x[global_b * in_features + chunk_offset + col]; } x_smem[b * X_SMEM_STRIDE + col] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (valid) { #pragma unroll for (int c = 0; c < CHUNK_SIZE; ++c) { float xval = x_smem[batch_in_block * X_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; #pragma unroll for (int p = 0; p < PAIRS_PER_THREAD; ++p) { int row0 = (pair_start + p) * 2; float w0 = W_smem[row0 * W_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; float w1 = W_smem[(row0 + 1) * W_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; acc[p * 2] += xval * w0; acc[p * 2 + 1] += xval * w1; } } } __syncthreads(); } // Handle remainder if in_features % CHUNK_SIZE != 0 int remainder = in_features - num_chunks * CHUNK_SIZE; if (remainder > 0) { int chunk_offset = num_chunks * CHUNK_SIZE; // Similar load but only `remainder` columns int W_total = PAIRS_PER_BLOCK * 2 * remainder; for (int i = tid; i < W_total; i += blockDim.x) { int row = i / remainder; int col = i - row * remainder; int global_row = pair_base * 2 + row; int global_col = chunk_offset + col; float val = 0.0f; if (global_row < out_features && global_col < in_features) { val = W[global_row * in_features + global_col]; } W_smem[row * W_SMEM_STRIDE + col] = val; } for (int i = tid; i < BATCH_PER_BLOCK * remainder; i += blockDim.x) { int b = i / remainder; int col = i - b * remainder; int global_b = batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK + b; float val = 0.0f; if (global_b < batch_size && chunk_offset + col < in_features) { val = x[global_b * in_features + chunk_offset + col]; } x_smem[b * X_SMEM_STRIDE + col] = val; } __syncthreads(); if (valid) { for (int c = 0; c < remainder; ++c) { float xval = x_smem[batch_in_block * X_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; for (int p = 0; p < PAIRS_PER_THREAD; ++p) { int row0 = (pair_start + p) * 2; float w0 = W_smem[row0 * W_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; float w1 = W_smem[(row0 + 1) * W_SMEM_STRIDE + c]; acc[p * 2] += xval * w0; acc[p * 2 + 1] += xval * w1; } } } __syncthreads(); } // Initialize s_sum if (tid < BATCH_PER_BLOCK) { s_sum[tid] = 0.0f; } __syncthreads(); if (valid) { float local_sum = 0.0f; #pragma unroll for (int p = 0; p < PAIRS_PER_THREAD; ++p) { int pair_idx = pair_base + pair_start + p; if (pair_idx * 2 < out_features) { float y0 = acc[p * 2]; float y1 = acc[p * 2 + 1]; if (pair_idx * 2 + 1 < out_features) { y0 += bias[pair_idx * 2]; y1 += bias[pair_idx * 2 + 1]; local_sum += fmaxf(y0, y1); } else { y0 += bias[pair_idx * 2]; local_sum += y0; } } } atomicAdd(&s_sum[batch_in_block], local_sum); } __syncthreads(); if (tid < BATCH_PER_BLOCK) { int global_batch = batch_tile * BATCH_PER_BLOCK + tid; if (global_batch < batch_size) { out[global_batch] = s_sum[tid] * scale_factor; } } } """ cpp_source = """ torch::Tensor forward_cuda( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor weight, torch::Tensor bias, float scale_factor ) { int batch_size = x.size(0); int in_features = x.size(1); int out_features = weight.size(0); auto result = torch::zeros({batch_size}, x.options()); const int BATCH_PER_BLOCK = 32; const int PAIRS_PER_BLOCK = 64; const int CHUNK_SIZE = 128; int num_batch_tiles = (batch_size + BATCH_PER_BLOCK - 1) / BATCH_PER_BLOCK; int num_pairs = (out_features + 1) / 2; int num_pair_tiles = (num_pairs + PAIRS_PER_BLOCK - 1) / PAIRS_PER_BLOCK; dim3 grid(num_batch_tiles, num_pair_tiles); dim3 block(256); int W_smem = PAIRS_PER_BLOCK * 2 * (CHUNK_SIZE + 1); int x_smem = BATCH_PER_BLOCK * (CHUNK_SIZE + 1); int total_smem = (W_smem + x_smem + BATCH_PER_BLOCK) * sizeof(float); AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES(x.scalar_type(), "fused_kernel", [&] { fused_kernel<CHUNK_SIZE><<<grid, block, total_smem>>>( x.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), weight.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), bias.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), result.data_ptr<scalar_t>(), batch_size, in_features, out_features, scale_factor ); }); return result; } """ ``` Wait, I used `AT_DISPATCH_FLOATING_TYPES`. Actually, to keep it simple, I'll cast explicitly: ```cpp torch::Tensor forward_cuda(...) { auto result = torch::zeros({batch_size}, x.options()); dim3 grid(...); dim3 block(256); int total_smem = ...; fused_kernel<64, float><<<grid, block, total_smem>>>( x.data_ptr<float>(), weight.data_ptr<float>(), bias.data_ptr<float>(), result.data_ptr<float>(), ... ); return result; } ``` But I should add a check for `x.device().is_cuda()` and scalar type. The eval probably runs on CUDA. Actually, maybe I should avoid dispatch and just use `float`. The reference uses `torch.rand` which defaults to float32. Let me write a simpler version without AT_DISPATCH to reduce compile time. Wait, I also need to handle the case where `bias` might be None if `nn.Linear(bias=False)`. But the reference uses `nn.Linear(in_features, out_features)` which defaults to `bias=True`. OK, I think I'll just write the explicit float version. If needed, I can cast. One more thing: in the kernel, can `pair_base * 2 + row` overflow an int? pair_base max = (32768/2 / 64 - 1) * 64 = 255 * 64 = 16320. pair_base * 2 = 32640. row max = 127. global_row max = 32767. Fits in int. But `global_row * in_features` = 32767 * 32768 = 1,073,676,296. This is close to 2^30 (1,073,741,824). It fits in a signed 32-bit int (max 2,147,483,647). But to be safe with larger sizes, I might want to use `int64_t` or `long long` for indexing. But for the given sizes, `int` is fine. Actually, I'll use `int64_t` for global indices to be safe: ```cuda int64_t global_row = pair_base * 2 + row; int64_t global_col = chunk_offset + col; int64_t idx = global_row * in_features + global_col; W_smem[...] = W[idx]; ``` This prevents overflow for larger sizes at a small cost. Similarly for x: `global_b * in_features + global_col`. OK, final code: I'll write the CUDA kernel carefully, then compile it. Let me write the full source string now.

turn 5llm latency: 13.3s
reasoning
The correctness issue is likely that the kernel doesn't handle the accumulation correctly. The issue is that the summed output is shared across all blocks but each thread block only sees partial sums. Let me rethink. The problem: - Each block computes partial sums for a subset of output dimensions - But we need the FULL sum across ALL output dimensions So the atomicAdd in shared memory is only adding within a block, but that block only computes a subset of pairs. We need each block to output its partial sum, and then do a reduction across blocks. Let me change the approach: - Each block computes partial sums and writes them to a temporary output of shape [num_batch_tiles, BATCH_PER_BLOCK] - Then do a grid-level reduction Actually, simpler approach: 1. Have each block write its batch partial sums to global memory 2. Do a separate reduction kernel or use atomicAdd to global memory Let me try: - Global atomicAdd for each batch element - Or: use two-pass approach with a workspace Actually for a cleaner approach, let me use grid synchronization or a workspace. Let me try global atomicAdd for each batch element:

Let me write a simple, correct postprocess kernel: ```cpp __global__ void postprocess_kernel(const float* y, float* out, int batch_size, int out_features, float scale) { int batch = blockIdx.x; int tid = threadIdx.x; int num_pairs = out_features / 2; float sum = 0.0f; for (int i = tid; i < num_pairs; i += blockDim.x) { float y0 = y[batch * out_features + 2 * i]; float y1 = y[batch * out_features + 2 * i + 1]; sum += fmaxf(y0, y1); } // Warp shuffle reduce for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { sum += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, sum, offset); } if (tid % 32 == 0) { atomicAdd(&out[batch], sum * scale); } } ``` Launch: 128 blocks of 256 threads each. Each block handles one batch. This is simple and should be correct. Actually, you know what, let me try one more approach. Instead of computing y = x @ W.T first (16.8MB write), what if we do the matmul on-the-fly in registers for each pair? The key difference from our first approach: we don't try to tile in 2D. Instead, each block processes one batch element, and each warp processes a chunk of output pairs. We keep x in shared memory (loading in chunks), and stream W from global memory. For batch_idx = blockIdx.x: - Load x[batch_idx, :] in chunks into SMEM - Each warp (32 threads) processes a subset of pairs - For each pair, each thread computes the dot product of x with W[2*pair] and W[2*pair+1] But this requires each thread to compute dot products, which means each thread reads W rows from global memory. Global memory access for W would be strided by in_features, which is terrible for coalescing. To coalesce W access, threads in a warp should read the same W row, different columns. But then each warp computes one pair at a time (all threads working on the same two W rows). With 32 threads per warp, that's only 2 outputs per warp, wasting a lot of compute. Hmm. What if each thread computes one dot product sequentially? Thread tid in warp computes pair = warp_id * PAIRS_PER_WARP + tid. It reads W[2*pair] and W[2*pair+1] from global memory. These are far apart in memory (separated by in_features*2), so not coalesced. Unless we transpose W first! If W is stored as (in_features, out_features) = (32768, 32768), then W[:, 2*pair] and W[:, 2*pair+1] are consecutive columns. Threads accessing consecutive pairs would access consecutive columns. But in our code, we call `self.linear.weight` which is (out_features, in_features). We'd need to transpose it. Actually, we can transpose W in the constructor: ```python self.register_buffer('W_t', self.linear.weight.t().contiguous()) ``` Then in the kernel, we access W_t which is (in_features, out_features). - W_t[:, 2*pair] is the transpose of W[2*pair, :] - For pair i, we need columns 2i and 2i+1 of W_t If thread tid accesses pair = base + tid, it accesses columns 2*(base+tid) and 2*(base+tid)+1. These are spread out, so memory access is strided by 2. Not great for coalescing unless we use wide loads. Actually, if we consider consecutive threads accessing consecutive columns, and we load them as float2, then thread tid loads columns (2*tid, 2*tid+1) for some base. That's actually perfectly coalesced if the warps are aligned properly! Wait, let me think. Suppose warp 0 processes pairs 0..31. Thread 0 accesses columns 0,1. Thread 1 accesses columns 2,3. ... For each K chunk of 64: - Threads collectively load W_t[k, 0:63] which is one row segment - Wait no, we need the full columns, not row segments. Actually, if W_t is (in_features, out_features), column-major in memory (since it's a transpose of row-major), then W_t[k, c] is at address k + c * in_features. So consecutive columns are very far apart. This is terrible for coalescing. So transposing W doesn't help for dot product access. We need row access of W, which means W should be in (out_features, in_features) format. Back to the original: W[pair, :] for row pair. Consecutive threads accessing different rows have strided access by in_features. Bad for coalescing. To coalesce W access, consecutive threads should read the SAME row, DIFFERENT columns. That means all threads in a warp work on the SAME pair. With 32 threads, each column is read by one thread. For a dot product, each thread computes partial sum over its columns, then warp reduces. This is BLOCKED GEMM style. For one pair (2 rows): - Warp reads W[2*pair, c] and W[2*pair+1, c] for c = tid, tid+32, tid+64, ... - Each thread accumulates dot(x, W[2*pair, :]) and dot(x, W[2*pair+1, :]) - After all K, warp reduce to get final dot products - Compute max and add to sum With 32 threads per warp and 128 warps per SM (max), we need many warps. For 16384 pairs and 128 batches: - Total pair computations = 128 * 16384 = 2,097,152 - If each warp handles one pair, we need 2,097,152 warps - With 32 threads per warp, that's 67M threads But we can parallelize over K as well by having each warp handle a K-subset of one pair. If PAIRS_PER_BLOCK=64 and total pairs = out_features/2 = 32768/2 = 16384, then pair_tiles = 16384/64 = 256. Not 128! I said pair_tiles=128 earlier. That was wrong. For B=32, P=64: - batch_tiles = 128/32 = 4 - pair_tiles = 16384/64 = 256 - num_blocks = 4 * 256 = 1024 Memory traffic: - Per block: x read = 32*32768, W read = 128*32768 - Total x = 1024 * 32 * 32768 = 1,073,741,824 = 4GB - Total W = 1024 * 128 * 32768 = 4,294,967,296 = 16GB - Total = 20GB At 3.35TB/s = 6ms. And total outputs = 1024 * 32 * 128 = 4,194,304. ✓ OK so with B=32, P=64, 1024 blocks, memory traffic is 20GB = 6ms. That's probably slower than the reference. What about B=64, P=128? - batch_tiles = 2 - pair_tiles = 128 - num_blocks = 256 - x read = 64*32768 per block - W read = 256*32768 per block - Total x = 256 * 64 * 32768 = 536,870,912 = 2GB - Total W = 256 * 256 * 32768 = 2,147,483,648 = 8GB - Total = 10GB = 3ms - SMEM = 64*65 + 256*65 = 20,800 floats = 83KB - Blocks per SM = 2 - Total concurrent = 264 > 256 This is better! Only 256 blocks, 10GB traffic, 83KB SMEM. Block assignment with 256 threads for B=64, P=128: - batch_in_block = tid / 4 (0..63) - pair_group = tid % 4 - PAIRS_PER_THREAD = 128 / 4 = 32 Each thread handles 1 batch and 32 pairs = 64 outputs. Total threads = 64 batches * 4 pair groups = 256. ✓ After K accumulation, each thread does max over 32 pairs and adds to s_sum. Total outputs = 256 blocks * 64 batches/block * 128 outputs/batch = 2,097,152? No wait: Block i computes 64 batches * 256 outputs = 16,384 outputs. 256 blocks * 16,384 = 4,194,304. ✓ Total FLOPs = 256 * 64 * 256 * 2 * 32768 = 274.9 GFLOPs. ✓ This looks like a solid configuration. Let me code this up. Wait, W_smem = 256 rows * 65 cols = 16,640 floats = 66.6KB x_smem = 64 batches * 65 cols = 4,160 floats = 16.6KB s_sum = 64 floats = 256B Total = 83.5KB Blocsk per SM = 228KB / 83.5KB = 2.7 → 2 blocks Total slots = 132 * 2 = 264 > 256 blocks All blocks can run. Each SM runs 2 blocks. But wait, 2 blocks per SM * 132 SMs = 264 concurrent blocks. We only have 256 blocks. So 8 slots are empty. That's fine. Actually each block has a lot of work: - 512 K chunks - For each chunk: load 256*64 W + 64*64 x, compute for all - After all: 32 max operations per thread The key question is whether 2 blocks per SM is enough to hide memory latency. 2 blocks * 256 threads = 512 threads per SM = 16 warps. On H100, you typically want 32-64 warps for good latency hiding. But H100 also has async copy and other features. Let's see. Actually, the blocks execute all K iterations sequentially with syncthreads between loads and compute. Within each block, 256 threads provide 8 warps, which is decent but might not fully hide global memory latency. If we can use 2 blocks per SM and they can overlap, that gives 16 warps. Still not ideal but might be OK. Alternatively, maybe I should use smaller blocks to get more occupancy. For example: - B=32, P=64: SMEM = 33KB + 8KB = 41KB, blocks per SM = 5, total concurrent = 660 > 1024? No, 660 < 1024. So 1024 blocks / 660 slots = about 2 waves. Each SM has 5 blocks * 256 threads = 1280 threads = 40 warps. That's good! But memory traffic was 20GB = 6ms. What's the tradeoff between occupancy and memory traffic? - B=32, P=64: 40 warps/SM, 6ms memory time - B=64, P=128: 16 warps/SM, 3ms memory time If the kernel is memory-bound, the 3ms one should win even with lower occupancy. What about a middle ground: B=32, P=128? - batch_tiles = 4, pair_tiles = 128 - num_blocks = 512 - SMEM = 256*65 + 32*65 = 18,720 floats = 75KB - Blocks per SM = 3 - Concurrent = 396 > 512? No, 396 < 512 - Waves = 512/396 = 1.3 waves - Warps per SM = 3 * 8 = 24 warps - Memory traffic = 512 * (32 + 256) * 32768 = 512 * 288 * 32768 = 4,831,838,208 floats = 18GB - 18GB / 3.35TB/s = 5.4ms Worse. What about B=32, P=32? - batch_tiles = 4, pair_tiles = 512 - num_blocks = 2048 - SMEM = 64*65 + 32*65 = 6,240 floats = 25KB - Blocks per SM = 9 - Concurrent = 1188 < 2048 - Waves = 2048/1188 = 1.7 waves - Warps per SM = 9 * 8 = 72 warps - Memory traffic = 2048 * (32 + 64) * 32768 = 2048 * 96 * 32768 = 6,442,450,944 floats = 24GB - 24GB / 3.35 = 7.2ms Worse. So B=64, P=128 seems like a good configuration. Let me write this kernel. I'll write it carefully. I'll include proper initialization, the K loop, remainder handling, local sum computation with max, shared memory reduction, and output writing. Let me also make sure I'm using the correct NNLinear weights format. nn.Linear stores weight as (out_features, in_features). So W[i,j] is row i, column j. For our tiled access: - W_smem stores rows pair_tile*256 to pair_tile*256+255 - Columns k*64 to k*64+63 - W is accessed as W[row * in_features + col] OK, let me write this now. I'll call it `fused_64_128`.
